<div id="review_43888883" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43888883" />
    <a title="Chana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1400289-chana"><img alt="Chana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373441206p2/1400289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43888883?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chana" href="/user/show/1400289-chana">Chana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1400289-chana?shelf=inspirational-memoirs">inspirational-memoirs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43888883" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6998282591118369661">Written in a sparse prose that is exactly right. There is the tragedy which is sudden and heartbreaking, but it happens in a moment and one can't go back. Listen to this, "I thought I had accepted Rich's accident, even though I kept putting myself in a place where it hadn't happened yet. Rich hadn't left for his walk. I could stop him at the door. I thought that not accepting meant turning my face to the wall, unable to function. So now today I look up the word acceptance and the definition is "</span>
  <span id="freeText6998282591118369661" style="display:none">Written in a sparse prose that is exactly right. There is the tragedy which is sudden and heartbreaking, but it happens in a moment and one can't go back. Listen to this, "I thought I had accepted Rich's accident, even though I kept putting myself in a place where it hadn't happened yet. Rich hadn't left for his walk. I could stop him at the door. I thought that not accepting meant turning my face to the wall, unable to function. So now today I look up the word acceptance and the definition is "to receive gladly" and that doesn't sound right. I flip to the back, and look up its earliest root "to grasp" and discover this comes from the old English for "a thread used in weaving" and bingo that's it. You can't keep pulling out the thread. You have to weave it in and then you have to go on weaving." That's exactly right isn't it? There is terrible sadness in this book, but there is more hope and future than sadness. I laughed often, tried to read some of it to my husband but it wasn't on his wavelength at all. I read it so far into the night that I was asleep before I put it down, and I picked it up immediately upon waking. As soon as I finished it I started it again. A treasure. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6998282591118369661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_404355136" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/404355136" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2453991-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548801525p2/2453991.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/404355136?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/2453991-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer404355136" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3684195420565522476">I got this book because of the endorsement by Stephen King...and this is the one time my favorite author has let me down.<br/><br/>This book is about Abigail Thomas, mostly. It's about how her husband is severely injured when he is hit by a car when walking their dog, which causes him to lose part of his brain and all of his short term memory. The story itself is compelling, but the way this story is told was not, for me at least.<br/>I guess the style of writing that Thomas entails is more stream of conscious</span>
  <span id="freeText3684195420565522476" style="display:none">I got this book because of the endorsement by Stephen King...and this is the one time my favorite author has let me down.<br /><br />This book is about Abigail Thomas, mostly. It's about how her husband is severely injured when he is hit by a car when walking their dog, which causes him to lose part of his brain and all of his short term memory. The story itself is compelling, but the way this story is told was not, for me at least.<br />I guess the style of writing that Thomas entails is more stream of consciousness - I had a hard time following the story because I was confused many times as to what the time period was. Also, while I guess it was at least a semi-autobiographical book, there were times that I really didn't care about what she was relaying to me. Interesting: she took a liking to paintings by disabled artists after seeing some paintings at her husbands care center. Not interesting: the entire story behind the many paintings she then goes on to accumulate, and why she is attracted to each. There was just a lot of information that I felt detracted from the actual story, and seemed more like impulsive rabbit trails. <br /><br />Not the worst book I've ever read, but not one I would recommend and I probably will not pick up future books by Thomas. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3684195420565522476" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1166532933" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17793560520800086720">I'm so glad I read this in 2015. That way I don't have to decide between <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8806553.House_of_Prayer_No__2_A_Writer_s_Journey_Home' title='House of Prayer No. 2 A Writer's Journey Home by Mark Richard' rel='nofollow noopener'>House of Prayer No. 2: A Writer's Journey Home</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/251789.A_Three_Dog_Life' title='A Three Dog Life by Abigail Thomas' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Three Dog Life</a> for <i>
  <strong>best book I've read all year.</strong>
</i><br/><br/>In Vivian Gornick's terms: The <i>
  <strong>situation</strong>
</i> is simple: Thomas's husband suffers severe brain trauma, such that he is always in the present moment. Always in the now. The past is no longer a memory, it never existed. <br/><br/>The <i>
  <strong>story</strong>
</i>, however is breathtaking. It's the story of adjusting to life, of love, of memory and loss </span>
  <span id="freeText17793560520800086720" style="display:none">I'm so glad I read this in 2015. That way I don't have to decide between <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8806553.House_of_Prayer_No__2_A_Writer_s_Journey_Home" title="House of Prayer No. 2 A Writer's Journey Home by Mark Richard" rel="nofollow noopener">House of Prayer No. 2: A Writer's Journey Home</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/251789.A_Three_Dog_Life" title="A Three Dog Life by Abigail Thomas" rel="nofollow noopener">A Three Dog Life</a> for <i>
  <strong>best book I've read all year.</strong>
</i><br /><br />In Vivian Gornick's terms: The <i>
  <strong>situation</strong>
</i> is simple: Thomas's husband suffers severe brain trauma, such that he is always in the present moment. Always in the now. The past is no longer a memory, it never existed. <br /><br />The <i>
  <strong>story</strong>
</i>, however is breathtaking. It's the story of adjusting to life, of love, of memory and loss of memory, of forgetting and remembering. It's an exploration into intimacy and comfort, into finding one's center. It's about perseverance and strength. And about finding grief you didn't even know existed. <br /><br />This could all be very heavy-handed, but for Thomas's sense of humor, irony and her delicate and deft way of handling words. If you have a heart of any kind: warm and loving, soft and mushy, or cold and crabby - it's a must read.<br /><br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2304393597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2304393597" />
    <a title="Elizabeth7781" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1929383-elizabeth7781"><img alt="Elizabeth7781" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420166385p2/1929383.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2304393597?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth7781" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth7781" href="/user/show/1929383-elizabeth7781">Elizabeth7781</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1929383-elizabeth7781?shelf=biographies">biographies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2304393597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14822868774518419904">“Suffering is the finest teacher. It teaches you details.” This excellent quote sums up what Abigail Thomas has learned in the power that is found in living a grateful life. She was faced with a truly life-altering blow and learned to appreciate what she still had vs mourning what was lost. Ms Thomas is honest with her readers. This isn’t easy. There are always those moments of tender loss that catch our hearts unaware and draw forth pangs. But for the most part, she has chosen her attitude and </span>
  <span id="freeText14822868774518419904" style="display:none">“Suffering is the finest teacher. It teaches you details.” This excellent quote sums up what Abigail Thomas has learned in the power that is found in living a grateful life. She was faced with a truly life-altering blow and learned to appreciate what she still had vs mourning what was lost. Ms Thomas is honest with her readers. This isn’t easy. There are always those moments of tender loss that catch our hearts unaware and draw forth pangs. But for the most part, she has chosen her attitude and it has paid dividends. <br /><br />I quite enjoyed her newfound definition of ‘capable’ (and I’m not about to spoil it for you). I chuckled at marking time by number of items knitted (clearly she has more discipline than I do. I get distracted by books!). But most of all, I reveled in her descriptive powers of one of the greatest pleasures of life - her dogs. Oh, I loved the dogs in this book. I wanted to curl up with them. I didn’t want to let them go by the end — and so I am starting Ms Thomas’ follow-on memoir, ‘What Comes Next and How To Like It’.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14822868774518419904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_147401184" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/147401184" />
    <a title="Louise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2143983-louise"><img alt="Louise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295627785p2/2143983.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/147401184?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Louise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Louise" href="/user/show/2143983-louise">Louise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2143983-louise?shelf=memoirs">memoirs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer147401184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17272772477304187954">Abigail and her husband Rich lived happily in Manhattan. On April 24, 2000 while Rich is out walking their dog, he is hit by a car and sustains a traumatic brain injury. His skull was shattered, his perception of time and reality forever altered. After being hospitalized, Rich is moved to a home where they had experience caring for people with traumatic brain injuries.<br/><br/>Abigail lives alone with her 3 dogs and visits Rich regularly but she never knows from one day to the next how she will find him.</span>
  <span id="freeText17272772477304187954" style="display:none">Abigail and her husband Rich lived happily in Manhattan. On April 24, 2000 while Rich is out walking their dog, he is hit by a car and sustains a traumatic brain injury. His skull was shattered, his perception of time and reality forever altered. After being hospitalized, Rich is moved to a home where they had experience caring for people with traumatic brain injuries.<br /><br />Abigail lives alone with her 3 dogs and visits Rich regularly but she never knows from one day to the next how she will find him. Rich has no short term memory whatsoever and can’t even remember what he did five minutes ago.<br /><br />In this memoir, Abigail captures not only the experience of watching a loved one slip into an unreachable world, but also the amazing strength and courage it takes to face life’s darkest nights alone. In reading this book it made me think about how well or not well I would handle my life after such a tragic loss. Lots to think about in this beautifully written memoir!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17272772477304187954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44721244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11359763793989908550">Abigail Thomas's memoir is an account of her life after her husband is hit by a car and suffers permanent brain damage. Thomas' struggle to redefine her existence parallels her husband's struggle to assign meaning, perspective, and context to the events happening around him. Her writing style is simple and rings true. One of my favorite passages reads: <br/><br/><blockquote>I thought that not accepting meant turning my face to the wall, unable to function. So now today I look up the word <i>acceptance</i> and the definition</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText11359763793989908550" style="display:none">Abigail Thomas's memoir is an account of her life after her husband is hit by a car and suffers permanent brain damage. Thomas' struggle to redefine her existence parallels her husband's struggle to assign meaning, perspective, and context to the events happening around him. Her writing style is simple and rings true. One of my favorite passages reads: <br /><br /><blockquote>I thought that not accepting meant turning my face to the wall, unable to function. So now today I look up the word <i>acceptance</i> and the definition is "to receive gladly" and that doesn't sound right. I flip to the back, and look up its earliest root, "to grasp", and discover this comes from the old English for "a thread used in weaving", and bingo, that's it. You can't keep pulling out the thread. You have to weave it in and then you have to go on weaving.</blockquote> </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13519314720145870829">This book had great potential, but turned out to be a woe-as-me tale of an over-privileged New York City hausfrau whose husband dies (he doesn't really die; he suffers a traumatic brain injury and loses his memory, but same difference for the purpose of this review.) <br/><br/>Anyway, there are glimpses that we'll explore that loss and how it fits into her relationships with her dogs, but instead we're stuck hearing about how she can't possibly manage to do the things her strong manly-man husband did (lik</span>
  <span id="freeText13519314720145870829" style="display:none">This book had great potential, but turned out to be a woe-as-me tale of an over-privileged New York City hausfrau whose husband dies (he doesn't really die; he suffers a traumatic brain injury and loses his memory, but same difference for the purpose of this review.) <br /><br />Anyway, there are glimpses that we'll explore that loss and how it fits into her relationships with her dogs, but instead we're stuck hearing about how she can't possibly manage to do the things her strong manly-man husband did (like change a tire and manage the bills, gag) in between nonchalant mentions of her fancy new car and chalet in the country. <br /><br />blech.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('siS5REbCD3pVHlBsYlbUXvmmZyHOI2MsOiojDvTZiXejYp8ciy1sZc+b0xBZynbs+LYwbVC/Q9JqvrBlFKO+zw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Then while living in Cambridge in her early 40s, “an age when the view from the hill can be clear and poignant both,” Caldwell adopts a rambunctious puppy named Clementine. On one of their bucolic walks, she meets Caroline and her dog, Lucille, and both women’s lives change forever.Though they are more different than alike, these two fiercely private, independent women quickly relax into a friendship more profound than either of them expected, a friendship that will thrive on their shared secrets, including parallel struggles with alcoholism and loneliness. They grow increasingly inseparable until, in 2003, Caroline is diagnosed with Stage IV lung cancer. Caldwell writes: “It’s an old, old story: I had a friend and we shared everything, and then she died and so we shared that, too.”     In her signature exquisite prose, Caldwell mines the deepest levels of devotion and grief in this wise and affecting account about losing her best friend. Let’s Take the Long Way Home is also a celebration of life and all the little moments worth cherishing—and affirms why Gail Caldwell is rightly praised as one of our bravest and most honest literary Rose Pastor arrived in New York City in 1903, a Jewish refugee from Russia who had worked in cigar factories since the age of eleven. Two years later, she captured headlines across the globe when she married James Graham Phelps Stokes, scion of one of the legendary 400 families of New York high society. Together, this unusual couple joined the burgeoning Socialist Party and, over the next dozen years, moved among the liveliest group of activists and dreamers this country has ever seen. Their friends and houseguests included Emma Goldman, Big Bill Haywood, Eugene V. Debs, John Reed, Margaret Sanger, Jack London, and W.E.B. Du Bois. Rose stirred audiences to tears and led strikes of restaurant waiters and garment workers. She campaigned alongside the country’s earliest feminists to publicly defy laws against distributing information about birth control, earning her notoriety as “one of the dangerous influences of the country” from President Woodrow Wilson. But in a way no one foresaw, her too-short life would end in the same abject poverty with which it began. By a master of narrative nonfiction, Rebel Cinderella unearths the rich, overlooked life of a social justice campaigner who was truly ahead of her time.<\/span>\n  <a His monu-mental album Frampt<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12179195457800120269\" style=\"display:none\">A revelatory memoir by rock icon and legendary guitarist Peter Frampton.\n\n\n\nDo You Feel Like I Do? is the incredible story of Peter Frampton\'s positively resilient life and career told in his own words for the first time. His monu-mental album Frampton Comes Alive! spawned three top-twenty singles and sold eight million copies the year it was released (more than seventeen million to date), and it was inducted into the Grammy Hall of Fame in January 2020.\n\nFrampton was on a path to stardom from an early age, first as the lead singer and guitarist of the Herd and then as cofounder -- along with Steve Marriott -- of one of the first supergroups, Humble Pie. Frampton was part of a tight-knit collective of British \'60s musicians with close ties to the Rolling Stones, the Beatles, and the Who. This led to Frampton playing on George Harrison\'s solo debut, All Things Must Pass, as well as to Ringo Starr and Billy Preston appearing on Frampton\'s own solo debut. By age twenty-two, Frampton was touring incessantly and finding new sounds with the talk box, which would become his signature guitar effect.\n\nFrampton remembers his enduring friendship with David Bowie. Growing up as schoolmates, crossing paths throughout their careers, and playing together on the Glass Spider Tour, the two developed an unshakable bond. Frampton also shares fascinating stories of his collaborative work with Harry Nilsson, Stevie Wonder, B. B. King, and members of Pearl Jam. He reveals both the blessing and curse of Frampton Comes Alive!, opening up about becoming the cover boy he never wanted to be, his overcoming sub-stance abuse, and how he has continued to play and pour his heart into his music despite an inflammatory muscle disease and his retirement from the road.\n\nPeppered throughout his narrative is the story of his favorite guitar, the Phenix, which he thought he\'d lost in a fiery plane crash in 1980. But in 2011, it mysteriously showed up again -- saved from the wreckage. Frampton tells of that unlikely reunion here in full for the first time, and why the miraculous reappearance is emblematic of his life and career as a quintessential artist.<\/span>\n  <a
